The Two Paths to Certainty in Islamic Thought
Buti enumerates two methodological paths to certainty in Islamic thought.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two methodological paths to certainty in Islamic thought—one through revelation and one through pure reason—and how they both lead to certainty and reinforce each other.

Core Idea
Certainty has two equally valid paths: revelation and reason, and they ultimately strengthen each other.

Buti explains that the Quran prompts reflection on unseen matters accessible to reason. He then outlines two paths that Islamic thought takes to attain certainty in matters of faith: one that begins with revelation and verifies its transmission, and another that relies on pure rational inquiry. Both paths are rigorous and lead to certainty, eventually converging to support one another.

Guided Path
1
Reflect on the Quran's role
Notice how Buti says the Quran impels us to reflect on unseen matters that the intellect can investigate, like God's existence and causality. This sets up why there are two paths.
The Quran is the starting point for both paths, even the rational one.
2
Understand the kalam scholars' choice
Buti notes that kalam scholars used pure reason not because revelation is insufficient, but to open another path to certainty alongside the truthful report.
This shows the complementary nature of the two paths.
3
Examine the first path: revelation-based
The first path begins with studying the phenomenon of revelation, then verifies the soundness of its transmission, leading to certainty because all premises are true.
This path relies on the truthful report but subjects it to rigorous verification.
4
Examine the second path: reason-based
The second path goes directly to pure rational proofs without delving into prophethood or the Quran's truthfulness. It produces certainty through rational demonstration.
This path does not ignore revelation but bypasses it as a starting point.
5
Appreciate the complementarity
Buti concludes that both paths lead to certainty and eventually meet and strengthen each other. They are not contradictory but mutually reinforcing.
This teaches us that reason and revelation are allies in Islamic thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غير أن من شأن القرآن مع ذلك أن يحملنا على التأمل والنظر في كل ما يخبرنا عنه ويحملنا على اليقين به من تلك الغيبيات التي يمكن للعقل البشري أن يجول ويلمس الحقيقة عنها كوجود الله عز وجل وحدوث الممكنات وجعلية الأسباب الكونية وماشابه ذلك. ولقد خاض علماء الكلام في بحث هذه المسائل عن طريق العقل والفكر المجرد دون أن يضعوا الخبر الصادق واسطة بينها وبينهم. ولكن لم يكن ذلك من أجل أنه السبيل الوحيد وإنما من أجل أن يشقوا إلى اليقين بها طريقاً أخرى من البحث إلى جانب طريق الخبر الصادق. وهكذا يسلك الفكر الإسلامي إلى الإيمان بوجود الله ووحدانيته ومتعلقات ذلك مسلكين اثنين كلاهما منهج علمي دقيق لاخدش فيه: أما المسلك الأول فيبدأ بمرحلة البحث في ظاهرة الوحي فإذا تجاوزها ثنى بمرحلة البحث في صحة النقل وتوفر مقومات اليقين فيه فإذا تجاوزها استيقن الأمر وصدقه لصدق كل مقدماته. وأما المسلك الثاني فيستعجل الطريق ويبحث في الأمر على هدى من الفكر المجرد والبراهين العقلية المحضة دون أن ينطلق بذهنه بعيداً إلى النبوة وحقيقتها والقرآن وصدقه. وكلا المسلكين ينتهيان بالباحث إلى اليقين بل إنها ليلتقيان أخيراً ليشد كل منهما من أزر الثاني.
However, it is the nature of the Quran, despite that, to impel us to reflection and consideration of everything it informs us about and impels us to certainty of, from those unseen things that the human intellect can roam and touch the truth about, such as the existence of Allah Almighty, the origination of possible things, the causal nature of cosmic causes, and the like. The scholars of kalam indeed delved into the discussion of these matters by way of the intellect and pure thought, without placing the truthful report as an intermediary between them and these matters. But that was not because it is the only way, but rather in order to open for themselves another path of research to certainty alongside the path of the truthful report. Thus Islamic thought proceeds to belief in the existence of Allah, His oneness, and related matters by two paths, both of which are a precise scientific methodology with no defect in them: As for the first path, it begins with the stage of research into the phenomenon of revelation; once it passes that, it proceeds to the stage of research into the soundness of transmission and the availability of the elements of certainty in it; once it passes that, it becomes certain of the matter and affirms it because all its premises are true. As for the second path, it hastens the way and researches the matter guided by pure thought and purely rational proofs, without projecting its mind far to prophethood and its reality and the Quran and its truthfulness. Both paths lead the researcher to certainty; indeed, they finally meet to strengthen each other.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage explains the Quran's role in prompting reflection on rationally accessible unseen matters and outlines the two methodological paths in Islamic thought. Buti first notes that the Quran impels us to reflect on unseen matters that the intellect can investigate, such as God's existence, the contingency of possible things, and causality. He then explains that kalam scholars studied these matters using pure reason without relying on the truthful report as an intermediary. However, this was not because reason is the only way, but to open another path to certainty alongside revelation. Buti then describes the two paths (maslakayn) of Islamic thought to faith in God's existence and oneness. Path one begins by investigating the phenomenon of revelation, then checks the soundness of transmission and the elements of certainty in it, leading to certainty because all premises are true. Path two proceeds directly with pure rational proofs without delving into prophethood or the Quran's truthfulness. Both paths conclude with certainty and eventually reinforce each other. The passage emphasizes the legitimacy and precision of both methods, and that they are complementary, not contradictory.

Key Terms
الفكر المجرد (pure thought)
البراهين العقلية (rational proofs)
المسلك الأول (first path)
المسلك الثاني (second path)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the path of reason and the path of revelation are opposed.
Correction: Buti emphasizes that both paths are valid, rigorous, and ultimately meet to strengthen each other. They are complementary, not contradictory.

Checkpoint
According to Buti, what is the relationship between the two paths to certainty?
A) They are contradictory
B) The path of revelation is superior
C) They both lead to certainty and strengthen each other
D) Only the path of pure reason is valid

Key Takeaway
Islamic thought offers two rigorous paths to certainty—revelation-based and reason-based—which ultimately converge to reinforce each other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Implicative Signification: A Rational Method Beyond Texts
Buti explains the first of two rational methods: implicative signification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how implicative signification (dalalat al-iltizam) functions as a rational method for determining truths not covered by explicit mutawatir texts, and why complete induction is essential for its validity.

Core Idea
Implicative signification requires exhaustive observation, not mere correlation.

When no explicit certain text addresses a matter, reason must take the lead. Buti introduces one of two rational methods: implicative signification, a tool for inferring one thing from another through constant correlation. This lesson unpacks how that correlation is established and why it demands complete induction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the translation. Note that the passage cuts off mid-sentence due to a page break.
The passage ends abruptly; we only have the first method explained.
2
Identify the two key components
Find where Buti defines implicative signification and where he explains complete induction. Notice the examples he gives.
3
Ask: Why is complete induction necessary?
Reflect on why a single observation is insufficient. Buti emphasizes examining all states and circumstances to confirm constant concomitance.
This guards against the misconception that correlation implies causation without full evidence.
4
Consider the implications
Think about how this method applies beyond texts. For instance, how might you use it to derive rulings or truths in Islamic theology?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما مالم يتعرض له الخبر المتواتر اليقيني بأي نص واضح صريح فينحصر السبيل إلى معرفة الحق فيه بالنظر العقلي وحده وهو يتحقق بمسلكين اثنين: المسلك الأول اتباع ما يسمونه بدلالة الالتزام: وهي أن يطرد ترابط بين شيئين بحيث إذا تأملت أحدهما تصورت الآخر وإنما يتم ذلك بعد أن يشهد له الاستقراء التام وهو أن تتبع الحالات والظروف المختلفة كلها لوجود هذين الشيئين فتجدهما متلازمين دائماً وذلك كدلالة النحول الشديد على المرض وكدلالة المآذن في البلدة على
As for what the certain mutawatir report does not address with any clear explicit text, the way to know the truth about it is confined to rational consideration alone, and it is realized by two methods: The first method is following what they call implicative signification (dalalat al-iltizam). It is that a correlation between two things is constant such that when you contemplate one, you conceive the other. This only occurs after complete induction (al-istiqra' al-tamm) testifies to it, which is that you examine all different states and circumstances of the existence of these two things, and you find them constantly concomitant. For example, the indication of severe emaciation for disease, and the indication of minarets in the town for
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage addresses the case when no explicit text from a certain mutawatir report covers a matter. In such cases, the only way to know the truth is rational consideration (al-nazar al-'aqli). Buti states that rational consideration is realized by two methods (maslakayn). He only elaborates the first method here: implicative signification (dalalat al-iltizam). This method relies on a constant correlation between two things: whenever you contemplate one, you automatically conceive the other. But this constancy requires complete induction (al-istiqra' al-tamm) — examining all different states and circumstances of the two things and finding them always concomitant. Buti gives two examples: the indication of severe emaciation for disease (i.e., emaciation implies the presence of a disease), and the indication of minarets in a town for a mosque (i.e., minarets imply that there is a mosque). Although the passage cuts off mid-sentence, it introduces the concept of implicative signification as a rational method for achieving certainty when scriptural texts are absent. The second method is not stated in this passage.

Key Terms
دلالة الالتزام
الاستقراء التام
النظر العقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a few observations are sufficient to establish a constant correlation for implicative signification.
Correction: Buti explicitly requires complete induction (استقراء تام) — examining all states and circumstances – not a limited sample.

Checkpoint
According to Buti, what must testify to the constant correlation for implicative signification to be valid?
A) A single example
B) Complete induction
C) Consensus of scholars
D) A clear scriptural text

Key Takeaway
Implicative signification is a rational method that yields certainty only when supported by complete induction demonstrating a constant, exceptionless correlation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold about Islam that is not directly from a clear, explicit text. Have you personally ensured it is supported by the kind of complete induction Buti describes? If not, what does that mean for the certainty of that belief? Will you take a concrete step this week to verify it?
?
Consider a time you drew a conclusion from observing a pattern in your life or in others (e.g., 'good deeds always lead to happiness'). Did you examine all possible states and circumstances before reaching that conclusion? How might your life change if you applied Buti's standard of exhaustive investigation before making such inferences?

The Revelation-Based Path to Certainty
Describes the first path: revelation-based investigation leading to certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first methodological path to certainty about God's existence, which begins with investigating revelation and then verifying the authenticity of transmission.

Core Idea
Certainty begins with examining the truth of revelation.

In this lesson, we explore the first of two paths that Islamic thought offers for attaining certainty about God's existence. This path starts with the phenomenon of revelation and proceeds through careful verification of transmitted reports. The author emphasizes that this method is rigorous and leads to certainty when its premises are sound.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the first path
Read the Arabic passage describing the first path. Notice the three stages mentioned.
The passage begins with a general introduction about two paths, then focuses on the first.
2
Identify the three stages
The first path involves three stages: investigation of revelation, then investigation of transmission and its certainty components, then arriving at certainty. Identify each stage.
The author uses 'إذا تجاوزها' to mark progression.
3
Understand the rigor of the method
The author states that this path is 'a precise scientific method with no flaw'. Reflect on what makes it rigorous.
4
Ask: Why start with revelation?
Consider why the path begins with the phenomenon of revelation rather than directly with proofs. The author implies it is a valid starting point.
This connects to the broader debate about sources of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المجرد دون أن يضعوا الخبر الصادق واسطة بينها وبينهم . ولكن لم يكن ذلك من أجل أنه السبيل الوحيد ، وإنما من أجل أن يشقوا إلى اليقين بها طريقاً أخرى من البحث، إلى جانب طريق الخبر الصادق وهكذا يسلك الفكر الإسلامي إلى الإيمان بوجود الله ووحدانيته ومتعلقات ذلك مسلكين اثنين ، كلاهما منهج علمي دقيق لاخدش فيه : أما المسلك الأول فيبدأ بمرحلة البحث في ظاهرة الوحي ، فإذا تجاوزها ، ثنى بمرحلة البحث في صحة النقل وتوفر مقومات اليقين فيه ، فإذا تجاوزها : استيقن الأمر وصدقه لصدق كل مقدماته • وأما المسلك الثاني فيستعجل الطريق ، ويبحث في الأمر على هدى من الفكر المجرد والبراهين العقلية المحضة ، دون أن ينطلق بذهنه بعيداً إلى النبوة وحقيقتها والقرآن وصدقه وكلا المسلكين ينتهيان بالباحث إلى اليقين ، بل إنها ليلتقيان أخيراً ليشد كل منهما من أزر الثاني .
Pure [reason] without placing the truthful report as an intermediary between them and [these matters]. But that was not because it [i.e., rational reflection] is the only path, but rather in order to open for themselves another way to certainty through investigation, alongside the path of truthful report. Thus Islamic thought takes, for belief in the existence of God, His oneness, and related matters, two paths, both of which are a precise scientific method with no flaw: As for the first path, it begins with the stage of investigation into the phenomenon of revelation; if it passes that, it then proceeds to the stage of investigation into the authenticity of transmission and the availability of the components of certainty therein; if it passes that, it becomes certain of the matter and affirms it because of the truth of all its premises. As for the second path, it hastens the way and investigates the matter guided by pure thought and purely rational proofs, without extending its mind far to prophethood and its reality and the Qur'an and its truth. Both paths lead the researcher to certainty. In fact, they ultimately meet to strengthen each other.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage presents two methodological paths to certainty about God's existence, oneness, and related doctrines. The author begins by noting that rational reflection is not the sole route; it exists alongside the path of truthful report (al-khabar al-ṣādiq). The first path starts with investigation of revelation (waḥy), then moves to verifying the authenticity of transmission (naql) and the elements of certainty (yaqīn). If all premises are sound, certainty is achieved. The second path proceeds directly through pure rational reasoning (al-fikr al-mujarrad) and rational proofs (al-barāhīn al-ʿaqliyya) without relying on prophethood or the Qur'an. The key point is that both paths are rigorous and ultimately converge, reinforcing each other. This guards against the misconception that one path invalidates the other.

Key Terms
اليقين
الخبر الصادق
الوحي
النقل

D) Arriving at certainty directly

Key Takeaway
The first path to certainty proceeds through investigation of revelation and transmission, culminating in certainty when premises are true.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The path described ends with certainty only when all premises are true. Are you willing to hold your beliefs to that standard, even if it means uprooting comfortable assumptions? What is one belief you need to re-examine today?

Describes the second path (pure rational reflection) and concludes that both paths converge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second path to certainty through pure rational reflection and how it converges with the revelation-based path.

Core Idea
Pure reason leads to the same certainty as revelation.

This lesson continues our exploration of the two paths to certainty. Here we examine the second path, which relies solely on rational proofs without reference to prophethood or scripture. The author concludes by highlighting that both paths ultimately meet and reinforce each other.

The Method of Dalālat al-Iltizām (Signification by Entailment)
Defines signification by entailment with examples and clarifies its non-causal basis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of dalālat al-iltizām as a rational inferential method based on constant concomitance, distinct from causal inference, and applicable to matters not addressed by definitive texts.

Core Idea
Inference by entailment relies on constant accompaniment, not causation.

This lesson introduces a key rational method for arriving at certain knowledge in matters where no definitive text exists: signification by entailment (dalālat al-iltizām). The author explains how through exhaustive induction we observe constant concomitance between two phenomena, allowing us to infer one from the other even when the signified is unseen. This method is fundamental in Islamic theology for arguments about the unseen.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the detailed examples and the clear distinction from causality.
2
Identify the definition and its conditions
Find the definition of dalālat al-iltizām and the condition of exhaustive induction (istiqrā' tāmm). Understand that the correlation must be persistent and universally observed.
The author says 'after exhaustive induction attests to it'.
3
Analyze the examples
Look at each of the four examples: emaciation/illness, minarets/Islam, siren/fire, unruly behavior/intoxication. Note that in each, the signifier is not the cause of the signified.
The author explicitly states 'the signifier is not a cause of the signified'.
4
Understand the inference process
The passage explains that through repeated observation of constant accompaniment, a 'signification bond' is formed. Then, when you observe the signifier, reason compels you to believe in the signified even if unseen.
The ambulance siren example illustrates this: you infer a patient without seeing them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما مالم يتعرض له الخبر المتواتر اليقيني بأي نص واضح صريح ، فينحصر السبيل إلى معرفة الحق فيه بالنظر العقلي وحده ، وهو يتحقق بمسلكين اثنين : المسلك الأول : اتباع ما يسمونه بدلالة الالتزام : وهي أن يطرد ترابط بين شيئين بحيث إذا تأملت أحدهما تصورت الآخر وإنما يتم ذلك بعد أن يشهد له الاستقراء التام ، وهو أن تتبع الحالات والظروف المختلفة كلها لوجود هذين الشيئين ، فتجدهما متلازمين دائماً وذلك كدلالة النحول الشديد على المرض ، وكدلالة المآذن في البلدة على إسلام أهلها ، وكدلالة صوت الصفارة الخاصة بعربة الإطفاء على حدوث حريق ، وكدلالة عربدة الرجل في الطريق على أنه قد شرب مسكراً . فالدال في مثل هذه الأمثلة كلها ليس علة للمدلول ، حتى نقول إن دلالته عليه من قبيل دلالة العلة على المعلول ؛ إذ النحول ليس علة للمرض ، والمآذن الصاعدة ليست علة لإسلام أهل البلدة، وصوت الصفارة ليس علـة لحدوث الحريق ، وعربدة الرجل ليست هي العلة لشربه المسكر وأنت عندما تشاهد الدالّ في هذه الأمثلة لا تبصر معها المدلول ولا تشاهده حتى تقول إن الدليل هو الرؤية والمشاهدة ، بل هو شيء خفي عن مشاهدتك وإحساسك إذاً فكيف دلت هذه الأشياء على مدلولاتها ، وكيف نؤمن بها دون أن نراها ؟! إن سبيل الدلالة أنها عُرفت بملازمتها الدائمة لملزوماتها . وتكرر ذلك دون تخلف ، وتم على ذلك الاستقراء ، فتكونت من هذه المقارنة الدائمة رابطة دلالة سارية بينهما وسبيل الاستفادة من هذا البرهان يكون ، بأن تتأمل في ظاهرة ما ، تشاهدها أمامك ، فإن رأيت - عن طريق دلالة الاستقراء - أن تلك الظاهرة تستلزم حقيقة معينة ، كان من الطبيعي في ميزان العقل أن تؤمن بها ، ولو لم تجدها ماثلة أمام عينيك ؛ فإن الذي يرى سيارة الإسعاف تنهب الأرض وهي بصفيرها المتواصل ، لا يشك أن ثمة مريضاً يعاني من حالة خطيرة على حياته وإن لم يكن يراه بعينيه ، بل لعله لا ينتبه الحقيقة السيارة التي تمر أمام عينيه بمقدار ما ينتبه لحالة المريض التي تقفز إذ ذاك إلى ذهنه وإذا طرح أحدهم أمامك دعوى يزعمها ، فإنك كثيراً ما تستطيع بواسطة -
As for what the certain mutawātir report does not address with any clear, explicit text, the way to knowledge of the truth therein is confined to rational reflection alone, and it is realized by two paths: The first path: following what they call signification by entailment (dalālat al-iltizām): It is that a correlation persists between two things such that when you contemplate one, you conceive the other. This occurs only after exhaustive induction (al-istiqrā' al-tāmm) attests to it, i.e., you trace all the different states and circumstances of the existence of these two things, and find them constantly concomitant. Examples: the signification of severe emaciation of illness, the signification of minarets in a town of its inhabitants' Islam, the signification of the fire engine's siren of a fire occurring, and the signification of a man's raucous behavior in the street that he has consumed intoxicants. In all such examples, the signifier is not a cause of the signified, so that we would say its signification is of the type of the cause's signification of the effect; for emaciation is not a cause of illness, rising minarets are not a cause of the inhabitants' Islam, the siren sound is not a cause of the fire, and the man's raucous behavior is not the cause of his drinking intoxicants. When you observe the signifier in these examples, you do not see the signified along with it, nor do you observe it, so that you would say the evidence is sight and observation; rather it is something hidden from your observation and sensation. So how did these things signify their signifieds, and how do we believe in them without seeing them? The way of signification is that they were known by their constant accompaniment of their concomitants. That recurred without exception, and induction was performed on that, and from this constant comparison a pervasive signification bond was formed between them. The way to benefit from this proof is that you contemplate a phenomenon you observe before you. If you see—through the indication of induction—that that phenomenon entails a specific reality, it is natural in the balance of reason to believe in it, even if you do not find it present before your eyes. For one who sees an ambulance racing along with its continuous siren does not doubt that there is a patient suffering from a life-threatening condition, even if he does not see him with his eyes. In fact, he may barely notice the car passing before his eyes as much as he attends to the patient's condition that jumps into his mind at that moment. And if someone presents you with a claim he asserts, you can often, by means of—
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage introduces the concept of 'dalālat al-iltizām' (signification by entailment) as a rational method for matters not covered by definitive transmitted texts (mutawātir). The author defines it as a persistent correlation between two things such that contemplating one leads to conceiving the other, established by exhaustive induction (istiqrā' tāmm)—i.e., observing all relevant cases and finding constant concomitance (mulāzama). Four examples are given: emaciation indicates illness, minarets indicate Islam, a fire engine's siren indicates fire, and unruly behavior indicates intoxication. Crucially, the signifier (al-dāll) is not a cause (ʿilla) of the signified (al-madlūl); the relation is not causal but one of constant accompaniment. The author then poses a rhetorical question: how do we infer the unseen from the seen? The answer is that through repeated observation of concomitance without exception, a 'signification bond' (rābiṭat dalāla) is formed. The practical application: when you observe a phenomenon, if inductive reasoning shows it entails a certain reality, reason compels you to believe in that reality even if unseen—like inferring a patient from an ambulance's siren. The passage ends with an incomplete sentence suggesting further examples. This lesson guards against confusing causal inference with inference by entailment.

Key Terms
دلالة الالتزام
الاستقراء التام
ملازمة
دال
مدلول
علية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Inference by entailment is the same as causal inference (the signifier causes the signified).
Correction: The author explicitly states the signifier is not a cause; the relation is based on constant accompaniment revealed by induction, not causation.

Checkpoint
What best describes the relation in dalālat al-iltizām according to the passage?
A) The signifier is the cause of the signified.
B) The signifier and signified are always causally linked.
C) The signifier is not a cause but constantly accompanies the signified.
D) The signified is always directly observable.

Key Takeaway
Dalālat al-iltizām allows rational belief in unseen realities through observed constant accompaniment, not causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see clear signs of divine care in your life—like provision after hardship—do you attribute it to your own efforts (a cause) or do you recognize the constant accompaniment of Allah's mercy (entailment)? How will you train yourself to see the entailment rather than claim causation?
?
The lesson teaches that through exhaustive induction, you can be certain of unseen realities. What is one spiritual reality about your own life (e.g., sincerity of intention, love of Allah) that you have doubted because you cannot see it? What constant outward signs would you need to examine—through honest self-induction—to arrive at certainty?
?
If you were to apply dalālat al-iltizām to your prayer, what outward signs (e.g., punctuality, peace afterward) are constantly present when your prayer is accepted? Are you looking for those signs or dismissing them as mere coincidence, thereby missing the hidden reality of Allah's acceptance? What will you change about how you evaluate your worship starting today?

The Signifier is Not a Cause
Buti enumerates examples to show signifier is not a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that in implicative reasoning, the signifier (al-dal) is not a cause of the signified (al-madlul), but rather a constant companion.

Core Idea
Implicative signification is not causal; the signifier accompanies, not causes.

Buti begins by clarifying a common misunderstanding about implicative reasoning. Many might think that when a sign points to something, it does so as a cause. Buti gives several everyday examples to show that the signifier is not the cause of the signified. This distinction is crucial for understanding the nature of implicative evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the examples carefully
Read the Arabic passage where Buti lists four examples: emaciation and illness, minarets and Islam, siren and fire, drunken behavior and drinking. Notice how each signifier is not the cause of the signified.
The examples are meant to be intuitive and familiar.
2
Identify the negation of causation
In each example, Buti explicitly denies that the signifier is a cause. Underline the phrase 'laysa 'illatan' (is not a cause) and see how he reinforces it.
3
Understand the problem posed
Buti then asks: if we don't see the signified directly, how do we know it? This sets up the need for an alternative explanation, which he provides in the next lesson.
This question drives the inquiry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالدال في مثل هذه الأمثلة كلها ليس علة للمدلول، حتى نقول إن دلالته عليه من قبيل دلالة العلة على المعلول؛ إذ النحول ليس علة للمرض، والمآذن الصاعدة ليست علة لإسلام أهل البلدة، وصوت الصفارة ليس علة لحدوث الحريق، وعربدة الرجل ليست هي العلة لشربه المسكر. وأنت عندما تشاهد الدالّ في هذه الأمثلة لا تبصر معها المدلول ولا تشاهده حتى تقول إن الدليل هو الرؤية والمشاهدة، بل هو شيء خفي عن مشاهدتك وإحساسك. إذاً فكيف دلت هذه الأشياء على مدلولاتها، وكيف نؤمن بها دون أن نراها؟!، إن سبيل الدلالة أنها عُرفت بملازمتها الدائمة لملزوماتها. وتكرر ذلك دون تخلف، وتم على ذلك الاستقراء، فتكونت من هذه المقارنة الدائمة رابطة دلالة سارية بينهما. وسبيل الاستفادة من هذا البرهان يكون، بأن تتأمل في ظاهرة ما، تشاهدها أمامك، فإن رأيت - عن طريق دلالة الاستقراء - أن تلك الظاهرة تستلزم حقيقة معينة، كان من الطبيعي في ميزان العقل أن تؤمن بها، ولو لم تجدها ماثلة أمام عينيك؛ فإن الذي يرى سيارة الإسعاف تنهب الأرض وهي بصفيرها المتواصل، لا يشك أن ثمة مريضاً يعاني من حالة خطيرة على حياته وإن لم يكن يراه بعينيه، بل لعله لا ينتبه الحقيقة السيارة التي تمر أمام عينيه بمقدار ما ينتبه لحالة المريض التي تقفز إذ ذاك إلى ذهنه. وإذا طرح أحدهم أمامك دعوى يزعمها، فإنك كثيراً ما تستطيع بواسطة دلالة الالتزام هذه أن تعلم صدقها أو بطلانها. وذلك عن طريق البحث عن مستلزمات تلك الدعوى، فإن رأيت هذه المستلزمات ماثلة أمامك كان ذلك دليلاً على صدق الدعوى، وإن كانت مفقودة أو كان الموجود نقيضها، كان ذلك دليلاً على كذب الدعوى فالرجل الذي يشرف بك على قرية ويقول لك: إن جميع أهلها مسلمون، لا يمكن أن تصدق كلامه هذا، إذا تأملت فلم تجد فوق بيوتها إلا صلبان الكنائس، على الرغم من أنك لم تجد أحداً من أهلها ولم تقف على اعتقاداتهم وما يدينون به عن طريق التجربة والمشاهدة. والذي يدعي لك أن منبع نشأة الفكر والعقل في الإنسان إنما هو شعوره بالحاجة إلى الغذاء، لا يمكنك أن تصدق دعواه، إذا تأملت في سائر الحيوانات التي تشترك مع الإنسان في الشعور بالحاجة إلى الغذاء دون أن يتكون لديها هي الأخرى شيء من التفكير والعقل.
The signifier in all such examples is not a cause of the signified, such that we say its signification of it is of the type of signification of a cause for an effect; for emaciation is not a cause of illness, and the rising minarets are not a cause of the Islam of the people of the town, and the sound of the siren is not a cause of the occurrence of the fire, and a man's drunken behavior is not the cause of his drinking intoxicants. And when you witness the signifier in these examples, you do not perceive the signified with it nor do you observe it, such that you would say the evidence is sight and observation; rather, it is something hidden from your observation and sensation. So how then did these things indicate their significates, and how do we believe in them without seeing them?! The way of signification is that it was known by their constant accompaniment of their concomitants. And that is repeated without exception, and induction has been carried out accordingly, so from this constant comparison a relation of signification arises between them. And the way of benefiting from this proof is that you reflect on a phenomenon that you witness before you, and if you see—by way of inductive signification—that that phenomenon entails a certain reality, it is natural in the balance of the intellect to believe in it, even if you do not find it manifest before your eyes; for he who sees an ambulance racing on the ground with its continuous siren does not doubt that there is a patient suffering from a critical condition threatening his life, even if he does not see him with his own eyes—indeed, perhaps he does not pay attention to the actual vehicle passing before his eyes as much as he pays attention to the patient's condition that jumps into his mind at that moment. And if someone puts forward a claim he asserts, you can often, by means of this implicative signification, know its truth or falsehood. That is by searching for the entailments of that claim: if you see these entailments manifest before you, that is evidence of the truth of the claim; and if they are absent or their opposite is present, that is evidence of the falsehood of the claim. The man who looks down upon a village and says to you: 'All its inhabitants are Muslims,' you cannot believe his statement if you reflect and find nothing above its houses but crosses of churches, despite the fact that you have not met any of its inhabitants nor learned of their beliefs and what they adhere to through experiment and observation. And the one who claims to you that the source of the origin of thought and intellect in man is only his feeling of need for food, you cannot believe his claim if you reflect on the rest of the animals that share with man the feeling of need for food without there developing in them as well any thought or intellect.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the nature of implicative reasoning (dalala al-iltizam). The author begins by clarifying that the signifier (al-dal) in these examples is not a cause of the signified (al-madlul). He gives four examples: emaciation is not a cause of illness, minarets are not a cause of the inhabitants' Islam, a siren is not a cause of a fire, and drunken behavior is not a cause of drinking. He then notes that when we observe the signifier, we do not directly see the signified; the signified is hidden from our senses. The question arises: how do these things indicate their significates, and how do we believe in them without seeing them? The answer is that the signification occurs through constant accompaniment (mulazama) and repeated observation without exception (istiqra'). This establishes a relation of signification between the two. The author then provides a method for using this proof: when you observe a phenomenon that entails a certain reality, reason compels you to believe in that reality even if it is not directly seen. He illustrates with the example of an ambulance's siren indicating a patient. Next, he applies this to evaluating claims: by examining the entailments (mustalzimat) of a claim, if they are present, the claim is true; if absent or opposite, it is false. He gives two examples: a man claiming a village is Muslim while seeing crosses on houses, and a claim that the origin of thought is the need for food, refuted by animals sharing that need without developing thought. The entire passage shows that implicative reasoning is a valid and essential tool for knowledge, based on induction and constant conjunction.

Key Terms
دلالة الالتزام
دال
مدلول
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think that implicative reasoning is a form of causal reasoning, where the signifier causes the signified.
Correction: Buti explicitly states that the signifier is not a cause; it is merely a constant concomitant. The relationship is not cause-effect but constant accompaniment.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the examples Buti gives to show that the signifier is not a cause?
A) Emaciation and illness
B) Minarets and Islam
C) Clouds and rain
D) Siren and fire

Key Takeaway
In implicative reasoning, the signifier is not the cause of the signified; they are distinct realities linked by constant accompaniment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You see the signs of Allah's existence everywhere—the order of the universe, the intricacies of creation. Yet do you truly believe in the unseen based on that constant accompaniment, or do you secretly wish for direct sight? What does your hesitation reveal about the strength of your conviction?
?
Think of a claim you recently made or heard about someone else. Did you examine the outward entailments (signs) to judge its truth, or did you rely on assumptions and desires? How can you practice the method taught in this lesson to avoid falsehood in your judgments?
?
The ambulance siren immediately brings the patient to mind. When you see the signs of Allah's mercy or anger in your life, does your heart immediately turn to Him in gratitude or repentance? What prevents that instantaneous connection, and what will you do to strengthen it starting today?

Method of Learning
beginner
5 min
How Implicative Signification Works
Buti explains how constant accompaniment and induction establish signification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the relation between signifier and signified is established through constant accompaniment (mulazama) and induction (istiqra'), not direct observation.

Core Idea
Constant conjunction without exception creates the link between sign and signified.

After establishing that the signifier is not a cause, Buti explains the actual mechanism: constant companionship. Through repeated observation that whenever the signifier is present, the signified is present, and this never fails, we form a reliable relation. Induction (istiqra') solidifies this link. He illustrates with the example of an ambulance siren indicating a patient.

Guided Path
1
Read the explanation of mulazama
Read the part where Buti says 'إن سبيل الدلالة أنها عُرفت بملازمتها الدائمة لملزوماتها' (the way of signification is that it was known by their constant accompaniment). Understand that signification is based on constant association, not causation.
Mulazama means constant accompaniment.
2
Understand the role of induction
Notice that Buti mentions 'وتكرر ذلك دون تخلف، وتم على ذلك الاستقراء' (repeated without exception, and induction carried out). This means that the relation is confirmed by many instances without exception.
Induction (istiqra') is the process of generalizing from repeated observations.
3
Apply to the ambulance example
Read the ambulance example: the siren (signifier) indicates a patient (signified) even though we don't see the patient. This shows how we accept the hidden reality through constant conjunction.
The example illustrates the mechanism in everyday life.

Checkpoint
According to Buti, how is the relation between a signifier (like a siren) and its signified (like a patient) established?
A) Through direct sensory observation of the patient.
B) Through a causal connection where the siren causes the patient.
C) Through constant accompaniment and repeated induction without exception.
D) Through logical deduction from first principles.

Key Takeaway
The link between signifier and signified is built on constant companionship and induction, not on causality or direct sight.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about a core belief you hold about Allah—something you cannot see. Do you truly rely on the constant signs He has placed (like creation, provision, etc.) to infer His attributes, or do you sometimes feel your faith wavers because you want direct proof? What would it take for you to strengthen the 'accompaniment' between the signs and your conviction?
?
Buti gives the example of testing a claim by looking at its entailments. In your own life, consider a claim you reject—perhaps about religion, morality, or the purpose of life. Have you honestly examined what its entailments would be if it were true? Are you avoiding that examination because it might demand a change you are not ready to make?
?
When you see a disaster or a blessing, does your mind automatically infer the hand of Allah, or do you explain it away with natural causes? How can you train yourself to see the unseen Reality behind every visible sign, such that your faith becomes as certain as the belief that an ambulance siren means a patient?

Method of Learning

Testing Claims with Implicative Reasoning
Buti demonstrates how to use implicative reasoning to verify or falsify claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how implicative reasoning can be used to evaluate the truth or falsehood of a claim by examining its entailments.

Core Idea
Check a claim by looking for what it implies; if the implications are absent or opposite, the claim is false.

Buti extends the application of implicative reasoning to everyday claims. When someone makes a statement, we can test its truth by searching for its logical entailments (mustalzimat). If those entailments are observed, the claim is likely true; if they are missing or contradictory, the claim is false. He provides two concrete examples: a village claim and a theory about human intellect.

Guided Path
1
Read the method for testing claims
Read the part where Buti says 'وذلك عن طريق البحث عن مستلزمات تلك الدعوى' (that is by searching for the entailments of that claim). Understand that we look for what the claim logically implies.
Entailments are the necessary consequences or signs of the claim being true.
2
Analyze the village example
Read the example: a man claims all villagers are Muslims, but we see crosses on houses. The crosses (entailment of non-Muslim faith) contradict the claim. So the claim is false.
The crosses are a signifier of Christianity, not Islam.
3
Analyze the thought origin example
Read the example: someone claims thought originates from need for food. But animals share the need for food but lack thought. Thus, the entailment (that need for food leads to thought) is absent in animals, disproving the claim.
This is a counterexample using the same method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The way of signification is that it was known by their constant accompaniment of their concomitants. And that is repeated without exception, and induction has been carried out accordingly, so from this constant comparison a relation of signification arises between them. And the way of benefiting from this proof is that you reflect on a phenomenon that you witness before you, and if you see—by way of inductive signification—that that phenomenon entails a certain reality, it is natural in the balance of the intellect to believe in it, even if you do not find it manifest before your eyes; for he who sees an ambulance racing on the ground with its continuous siren does not doubt that there is a patient suffering from a critical condition threatening his life, even if he does not see him with his own eyes—indeed, perhaps he does not pay attention to the actual vehicle passing before his eyes as much as he pays attention to the patient's condition that jumps into his mind at that moment. And if someone puts forward a claim he asserts, you can often, by means of this implicative signification, know its truth or falsehood. That is by searching for the entailments of that claim: if you see these entailments manifest before you, that is evidence of the truth of the claim; and if they are absent or their opposite is present, that is evidence of the falsehood of the claim. The man who looks down upon a village and says to you: 'All its inhabitants are Muslims,' you cannot believe his statement if you reflect and find nothing above its houses but crosses of churches, despite the fact that you have not met any of its inhabitants nor learned of their beliefs and what they adhere to through experiment and observation. And the one who claims to you that the source of the origin of thought and intellect in man is only his feeling of need for food, you cannot believe his claim if you reflect on the rest of the animals that share with man the feeling of need for food without there developing in them as well any thought or intellect.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the nature of implicative reasoning (dalala al-iltizam). The author begins by clarifying that the signifier (al-dal) in these examples is not a cause of the signified (al-madlul). He gives four examples: emaciation is not a cause of illness, minarets are not a cause of the inhabitants' Islam, a siren is not a cause of a fire, and drunken behavior is not a cause of drinking. He then notes that when we observe the signifier, we do not directly see the signified; the signified is hidden from our senses. The question arises: how do these things indicate their significates, and how do we believe in them without seeing them? The answer is that the signification occurs through constant accompaniment (mulazama) and repeated observation without exception (istiqra'). This establishes a relation of signification between the two. The author then provides a method for using this proof: when you observe a phenomenon that entails a certain reality, reason compels you to believe in that reality even if it is not directly seen. He illustrates with the example of an ambulance's siren indicating a patient. Next, he applies this to evaluating claims: by examining the entailments (mustalzimat) of a claim, if they are present, the claim is true; if absent or opposite, it is false. He gives two examples: a man claiming a village is Muslim while seeing crosses on houses, and a claim that the origin of thought is the need for food, refuted by animals sharing that need without developing thought. The entire passage shows that implicative reasoning is a valid and essential tool for knowledge, based on induction and constant conjunction.

Key Takeaway
Implicative reasoning allows us to judge claims by observing whether their necessary implications are present or absent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If not, what does that say about your intellectual honesty? Will you now commit to testing one such claim today?
?
Buti says that if the opposite entailment is present, the claim is false. Consider the claim 'I am a true believer.' What signs (entailments) should be present in your daily life? Classification of Implicative Signification
Buti explains the threefold classification of dalala al-luzum with two types detailed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the threefold classification of implicative signification (dalala al-luzum) and the distinguishing features of the first two types: non-evident and evident in the general sense.

Core Idea
Implicative signification varies in evidentiary strength; some need proof, others just reflection.

In this passage, Buti explains that not all implicative relations yield certainty. The strength of an implication depends on how clearly the concomitant relationship is perceived. Scholars therefore divided implicative signification into three levels, from weakest to strongest. Here, we explore the first two types, which illustrate how some implications require external proof while others become clear upon careful reflection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the author's structure: an introductory statement, then a threefold division, then detailed description of the first two types.
The passage cuts off at the beginning of the third type; focus on the two complete types.
2
Identify the classification principle
Find the criterion used to divide implicative signification. The author says the division is based on 'the degree of clarity and independence from another proof'.
This criterion creates a hierarchy of evidentiary strength.
3
Understand the first type: non-evident implication
Study the definition and the triangle example. Why does this type require another proof? Why is it only part of a proof?
The triangle example shows that without geometric proof, the implication is not certain.
4
Understand the second type: evident implication (general sense)
Read the definition and the beginning example. Notice that it only requires conception and deep reflection, not an external proof.
The author mentions a contingent thing as an example, but the passage ends before full explanation.
5
Compare the two types
Contrast the two: one needs an external proof, the other is self-sufficient after reflection. Consider why this distinction matters for theological arguments.
This helps assess the strength of implicative arguments in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنواع دلالة الالتزام: إلا أن هذا التلازم لا يورث اليقين دائماً، إذ الأمر فيه منوط بمدى وضوح التلازم واستغنائه عن برهان آخر يدل عليه، ولذلك قسم العلماء دلالة اللزوم إلى ثلاثة أقسام، ترتقي في القوة من الأدنى إلى الأعلى: أولاها ما يسمى باللزوم غير البيّن: وهو أن يتوقف الجزم بوجود اللزوم فيه على إقامة برهان آخر، كالتزام زوايا المثلث لقائمتين، فإن العقل لا يجزم بذلك لكل مثلث مالم يطلع على برهان آخر مثبت له كتصور الدائرة ومعرفة درجاتها. ومن ثم فإن هذا التلازم وحده لا يعتبر دليلاً لأنه هو نفسه يحتاج إلى برهان ودليل عليه، ولكنه يعتبر جزءاً من دليل، يتكامل بضم تتمته إليه. ثانيها مايسمى باللزوم البيّن بالمعنى الأعم: وهو أن يتوقف إدراك اللزوم بين الشيئين على تصور كل منها والنظر فيه ملياً، كدلالة الشيء الممكن
Types of implicative signification: However, this concomitance does not always yield certainty, for the matter depends on the degree of clarity of the concomitance and its independence from another proof indicating it. Therefore, the scholars divided the signification of implication (dalala al-luzum) into three divisions, ascending in strength from the lowest to the highest: First of them is what is called non-evident implication (al-luzum ghayr al-bayyin): it is that judgement of the existence of the implication is dependent on establishing another proof, such as the implication of the angles of a triangle for two right angles; for the intellect does not judge that for every triangle unless it is informed of another proof affirming it, such as the conception of the circle and knowledge of its degrees. Therefore, this concomitance by itself is not considered a proof because it itself needs a proof and evidence for it, but it is considered a part of a proof, completed by adding its complement to it. Second of them is what is called evident implication in the general sense (al-luzum al-bayyin bi'l-ma'na al-a'mm): it is that perception of the implication between two things is dependent on conceiving each of them and deeply reflecting on them, such as the signification of a contingent thing...
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the types of implicative signification (dalala al-luzum). The author states that concomitance does not always produce certainty; it depends on how clear the concomitance is and whether it needs another proof. Therefore, scholars divided it into three types, ordered by strength. The first type is 'non-evident implication' (al-luzum ghayr al-bayyin): here, judgment of the implication requires another proof. The example is the triangle's angles summing to two right angles, which is not self-evident to the intellect without a geometric proof. This type alone is not a proof but a part of a proof. The second type is 'evident implication in the general sense' (al-luzum al-bayyin bi'l-ma'na al-a'mm): here, perceiving the implication requires conceiving both things and reflecting deeply on them. The author begins an example (a contingent thing) but the passage is cut off. The classification helps the student understand that different implicative arguments have different evidentiary weight, and some need additional support.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All implicative significations are equally certain.
Correction: Implicative signification varies in evidentiary strength: some types require external proof and are less certain, while others are self-evident after reflection.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes non-evident implication (al-luzum ghayr al-bayyin) from evident implication (al-luzum al-bayyin bi'l-ma'na al-a'mm)?
A) Non-evident implication is always false, while evident is always true.
B) Non-evident implication requires another proof to establish it, while evident only requires conceiving and reflecting on the two things.
C) Non-evident implication is based on sense perception, while evident is based on intuition.
D) Non-evident implication is specific to geometric truths, while evident applies only to physical objects.

Key Takeaway
The strength of an implicative argument depends on whether the implication is self-evident after reflection or requires an external proof; this classification helps assess its reliability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you accept a religious truth, do you ever stop to ask whether the evidence for it is self-evident after reflection or requires deeper proof? How often do you blindly accept without verifying the strength of the implication? Commit this week to examine one of your core beliefs and trace the proof for it using this classification.
?
The scholars divided implication into levels. In your own life, are there beliefs you hold that are based on non-evident implications that you have never complemented with proof? Ponder your knowledge of Allah's existence – is it based on clear implication or do you need to study more? What step will you take today to strengthen that proof.
?
Reflect on your own thought process: Have you ever assumed that something is necessarily true just because it 'feels' connected, without verifying the logical link? How might this classification help you become more precise in your understanding of Islamic beliefs and shield you from shaky arguments?

Implicative Signification and Its Verification Method
Defines implicative signification and its verification method with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how implicative signification (dalalat al-iltizam) works as a method for verifying claims by examining their necessary implications.

Core Idea
Implicative signification judges a claim by its necessary implications.

In this passage, the author introduces a powerful method for evaluating truth claims without direct observation: implicative signification. Instead of proving a claim directly, you examine its unavoidable consequences. If those consequences are present, the claim is likely true; if absent or opposite, the claim is false. The passage illustrates this with clear examples from village inhabitants and the origin of human thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument flow naturally.
Notice the concise style and the use of concrete examples.
2
Identify the method
Locate where the author states the general method of implicative signification. Understand the two conditions: presence of implications indicates truth; absence or opposite indicates falsehood.
The method is stated in the first two sentences.
3
Examine the first example
Consider the village example. How do the crosses on houses serve as an implication that contradicts the claim? This shows how observable implications can disprove a claim without direct verification.
4
Examine the second example
Consider the claim about thought arising from hunger. How does the existence of animals that feel hunger but lack thought refute the claim? This shows how implications from a broader set of facts can disprove a generalization.
5
Reflect on the concept
Think about how this method applies in everyday reasoning or theological arguments. Why is it important to identify necessary implications of a claim?
This helps avoid relying on direct evidence when it is unavailable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دلالة الالتزام هذه أن تعلم صدقها أو بطلانها . وذلك عن طريق البحث عن مستلزمات تلك الدعوى ، فإن رأيت هذه المستلزمات ماثلة أمامك كان ذلك دليلاً على صدق الدعوى ، وإن كانت مفقودة أو كان الموجود نقيضها ، كان ذلك دليلاً على كذب الدعوى فالرجل الذي يشرف بك على قرية ويقول لك : إن جميع أهلها مسلمون ، لا يمكن أن تصدق كلامه هذا ، إذا تأملت فلم تجد فـوق بـيـوتـهـا إلا صلبان الكنائس ، على الرغم من أنك لم تجد أحداً من أهلها ولم تقف على اعتقاداتهم وما يدينون به عن طريق التجربة والمشاهدة. والذي يدعي لك أن منبع نشأة الفكر والعقل في الإنسان إنما هو شعوره بالحاجة إلى الغذاء ، لا يمكنك أن تصدق دعواه ، إذا تأملت في سائر الحيوانات التي تشترك مع الإنسان في الشعور بالحاجة إلى الغذاء دون أن يتكون لديها هي الأخرى شيء من التفكير والعقل .
This implicative signification is that you know its truth or falsehood. And that is by investigating the implications of that claim. If you see these implications present before you, that is evidence of the truth of the claim. If they are absent or their opposite is present, that is evidence of the falsehood of the claim. So the man who makes you overlook a village and says to you: All its inhabitants are Muslims, you cannot believe his statement, if you ponder and find nothing above its houses but crosses of churches, despite not having seen any of its inhabitants and not having learned their beliefs and what they adhere to through experience and observation. And the one who claims to you that the source of the emergence of thought and intellect in humans is only their feeling of need for food, you cannot believe his claim, if you ponder on the other animals that share with humans the feeling of need for food without developing any of thought or intellect.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage defines implicative signification (dalalat al-iltizam) as the means to know the truth or falsehood of a claim by investigating its necessary implications (mustalzimat). It first states the general method: if the implications are present, the claim is true; if absent or opposite, false. Then it provides two examples. The first: a man claims all villagers are Muslims, but upon inspection you see only crosses on houses, implying they are not Muslims, even without direct observation. The second: a claim that thought arises only from the need for food is refuted by noting that animals share that need but lack thought. Both examples illustrate how implications can disprove claims without empirical verification of the claim itself.

Classification of Implicative Signification and the First Type: Non-Evident Implication
Classifies implicative signification into three types and describes the first: non-evident implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars classify implicative signification into three types and why the first type, non-evident implication, requires an external proof to establish certainty.

Core Idea
Implicative signification varies in strength; the weakest type needs another proof.

Not every implication automatically yields certainty. Scholars have categorized implicative signification based on the clarity and independence of the logical connection. The lowest category, non-evident implication, serves as a component of proof rather than a complete proof itself. This lesson introduces this classification and unpacks the first type.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the general principle
The passage begins by noting that implication does not always yield certainty; it depends on clarity. Understand this foundational idea.
2
Identify the three-type classification
Scholars divided implicative signification into three ascending tiers. Focus on the first: non-evident implication.
3
Examine the example of triangle angles
The example illustrates that the necessary connection (triangle angles summing to 180 degrees) is not obvious; it requires a geometric proof.
The example uses the circle method to prove the sum of angles.
4
Apply the conclusion
Recognize that non-evident implication alone is not a proof but a part of one, needing completion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنواع دلالة الالتزام : إلا أن هذا التلازم لا يورث اليقين دائماً ، إذ الأمر فيه منوط بمدى وضوح التلازم واستغنائه عن برهان آخر يدل عليه ، ولذلك قسم العلماء دلالة اللزوم إلى ثلاثة أقسام ، ترتقي في القوة من الأدنى إلى الأعلى : أولاها ما يسمى باللزوم غير البيّن : وهو أن يتوقف الجزم بوجود اللزوم فيه على إقامة برهان آخر ، كالتزام زوايا المثلث لقائمتين ، فإن العقل لا يجزم بذلك لكل مثلث مالم يطلع على برهان آخر مثبت لـه كتصور الدائرة ومعرفة درجاتها . ومن ثم فإن هذا التلازم وحده لا يعتبر دليلاً لأنه هو نفسه يحتاج إلى برهان ودليل عليه ، ولكنه يعتبر جزءاً من دليل ، يتكامل بضم تتمته إليه
Types of implicative signification: However, this implication does not always produce certainty, because the matter depends on the clarity of the implication and its independence from another proof indicating it. Therefore, the scholars divided implicative signification into three types, ascending in strength from lowest to highest: First: what is called non-evident implication: that certainty of the existence of the implication depends on presenting another proof, like the implication of the angles of a triangle being two right angles, for the intellect does not judge that for every triangle unless it learns of another proof proving it, such as conceiving the circle and knowing its degrees. Hence, this implication alone is not considered a proof because it itself needs a proof and evidence for it, but it is considered a part of a proof, completed by joining its complement to it.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage begins by noting that not all implications produce certainty; it depends on clarity and independence from another proof. Scholars classified implication into three types ascending in strength. The first type is luzum ghayr bayyin (non-evident implication). For this type, certainty of the necessary connection requires an additional proof. The example is the implication that the angles of a triangle equal two right angles; the intellect cannot judge this for every triangle without a geometric proof (e.g., using a circle and its degrees). Therefore, this implication alone is not a complete proof but a component that needs complementation.

Evident Implication in the Broader Sense
Defines and exemplifies the second type of implication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second type of logical implication—evident in the broader sense—where deep reflection on the meanings alone suffices to perceive the connection, without needing an extra proof.

Core Idea
Deep reflection on meanings reveals the connection; no extra proof needed.

After introducing the most direct implication, Imam al-Būṭī now presents the second type, which requires careful thought but not a separate proof. This type, 'evident in the broader sense,' shows how understanding key concepts like possibility and necessity naturally leads to recognizing relationships such as creation and eternity. Let us examine the definition and examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage has a definition followed by two examples.
2
Identify the definition
Find the sentence that defines the second type. What is the condition for perceiving the implication?
The condition is that you need to conceive each thing and examine it thoroughly.
3
Examine the first example
Consider the example of a possible thing (mumkin) implying it is created (hadith). Why does this require deep reflection?
You must understand what possibility means: that existence is not necessary and depends on a cause.
4
Examine the second example
Now consider the Necessary Existent (Wajib al-Wujud) implying eternity (qadim). How is this similar to the first example?
Both require you to grasp the meanings fully before the implication becomes clear.
5
Contrast with non-evident implication
Notice the author's closing remark: 'you do not need to conceive another proof'—this contrasts with the third type (non-evident) which will be introduced next.
This helps you understand the classification of implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانيها مايسمى باللزوم البيّن بالمعنى الأعم : وهو أن يتوقف إدراك اللزوم بين الشيئين على تصور كل منها والنظر فيه ملياً ، كدلالة الشيء الممكن على أنه حادث ، وكدلالة واجب الوجود على أنه قديم ، فإنك لاتفهم لزوم الممكنات لصفة الحدوث إلا إذا أمعنت النظر في معنى الإمكان ، وأدركت أنــه الشيء الذي لايحيل العقل فقدانه وإنما ترجح فيه جانب الوجود المرجح طارئ ، ثم أمعنت النظر في معنى الحدوث وتصورت الصلة بينه وبين كل الممكنات التي من شأنها أن توجد بتأثير غيرها ولكنك على كل حال لاتحتاج إلى تصور برهان آخر ( كما هو شأن اللزوم غير البين ) لثبوت هذا اللزوم
Second: what is called evident implication in the broader sense: that perceiving the implication between two things depends on conceiving each of them and examining them thoroughly, like the implication of a possible thing indicating that it is created, and the implication of the necessary existent indicating that it is eternal. For you do not understand the implication of possibles for the attribute of creation except if you delve into the meaning of possibility and realize that it is something whose absence the intellect does not deem impossible, but the aspect of existence is determined by a supervening determinant; then you delve into the meaning of creation and conceive the connection between it and all possibles that tend to exist through the influence of another. But in any case, you do not need to conceive another proof (as is the case with non-evident implication) to establish this implication.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage defines the second type of implication: luzum bayyin bil-ma'na al-a'amm (evident in the broader sense). To perceive this implication, one must conceive and thoroughly examine each of the two terms, but no additional proof is needed. The first example: a possible thing (mumkin) implies it is created (hadith), because possibility means its existence is not necessary but dependent on a cause. The second example: the Necessary Existent (Wajib al-Wujud) implies eternity (qadim). Understanding the meanings of 'possibility' and 'creation' reveals the connection. Unlike the first type, this does not require an external proof, only careful reflection.

Key Terms
لزوم بيّن بالمعنى الأعم
ممكن
حادث
واجب الوجود
قديم
إمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this type requires no thought; actually it requires deep reflection.
Correction: The passage says 'perceiving the implication depends on conceiving each of them and examining them thoroughly'—it is not immediate like the first type; you must reflect deeply on the meanings.

Checkpoint

A) Consult a separate proof
B) Conceive and deeply reflect on the meanings of possibility and creation
C) Observe empirical evidence
D) Rely on authority

Key Takeaway
Evident implication in the broader sense becomes clear through deep reflection on the meanings of the terms, without need for additional proof.

?
The Necessary Existent implies eternity. Do your daily actions reflect that Allah is the Eternal, or do you live as if He is temporary in your concerns? How would your priorities shift if you internalized that He alone is necessary and everlasting?

The Third Type: Evident Implication in the Narrower Sense
Defines the strongest implication type with examples and ranks it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what makes an implication 'evident in the narrower sense' and why it is the strongest type of implication in logical reasoning.

Core Idea
The strongest proof is when merely conceiving the sign suffices for certainty about what it implies.

We have seen three types of implication. Now we examine the strongest: luzum bayyin bil-ma'na al-akhass. In this type, the connection between sign and meaning is so clear that no further thought is needed. The mind immediately jumps to the conclusion. This passage defines it clearly with concrete examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and examples
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the key term 'luzum bayyin bil-ma'na al-akhass' and how the author describes it as requiring only the implicant to be conceived.
2
Analyze the examples
Consider each example: ambulance->patient, groaning->illness, voice in darkness->living being. Notice how in each case the connection is immediate and certain.
These examples are from natural and intellectual matters.
3
Compare with other types
The author ends by ranking the three types. Identify why this third type is strongest, and why the first type alone is not a proof.
This reinforces the hierarchy of implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثالثها اللزوم البيّن بالمعنى الأخص : وهو أن يكون تصور الملزوم وحده كافياً في تصور اللزوم والجزم به ، كدلالة سيارة الإسعاف على المريض في المثال السابق . وكدلالة الأنين على المرض، في القضايا الطبيعية ، ودلالة اللفظ المنبعث من شخص في الظلام على وجود كائن حي، في القضايا العقلية . فإن قوة اللزوم التي بين كل منهما ، تجعل العقل يتصور المرض بمجرد تصور الأنين . ويتصور الكائن الحي بمجرد سماع اللفظ المنبعث في الظلام ، دون حاجة إلى التفكر في الرابطة بينهما وهذا القسم الثالث أقواها من حيث الدلالة وقوة البرهان ، يليه القسم الثاني . أما القسم الأول وهو التلازم غير البيّن . فهو باستقلاله لا يعتبر برهاناً ، حتى يضاف إليه برهان آخر يكشف عن صدق التلازم كما ذكرنا
Third: the evident implication in the narrower sense: that conceiving the implicant alone is sufficient to conceive and be certain of the implication, like the implication of an ambulance indicating a patient in the previous example. And like the implication of groaning indicating illness in natural matters, and the implication of a word emanating from a person in darkness indicating the existence of a living being in intellectual matters. For the strength of the implication between each of them makes the intellect conceive the illness merely by conceiving the groaning, and conceive the living being merely by hearing the word emanating in darkness, without needing to reflect on the connection between them. And this third type is the strongest in terms of indication and strength of proof, followed by the second type. As for the first type, the non-evident implication, it alone is not considered a proof until another proof is added to it revealing the truth of the implication, as we mentioned.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage defines the third and strongest type: luzum bayyin bil-ma'na al-akhass (evident in the narrower sense). Here, merely conceiving the implicant (malzum) is enough to grasp the implication with certainty. Examples: an ambulance implies a patient; groaning implies illness (natural matters); a voice in darkness implies a living being (intellectual matters). The strength of the connection makes the intellect immediately jump to the implied meaning without reflection. The passage then summarizes the strength order: third type strongest, second type next, and the first type alone is not a proof until supplemented.

The Second Method: Al-Qiyas in Islamic Theology
Defines Islamic qiyas and distinguishes it from Greek logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what Islamic qiyas is and why it is distinct from the Greek logical syllogism.

Core Idea
Islamic qiyas is Quran-inspired, not Greek logic.

Dr. al-Buti introduces the second method of theological proof: analogy (al-qiyas). He immediately clarifies a common confusion: this is not the logical syllogism borrowed from Greek philosophy. Rather, it is the analogical reasoning developed by Islamic jurists and theologians, inspired directly by the Qur'an. Let's examine this distinction carefully.

Guided Path
1

The passage contrasts two things: the Greek logical syllogism and the Islamic analogy. Identify what each is based on.
Greek: propositions and figures. Islamic: inspiration from the Qur'an.
3

Note that Islamic qiyas comes from the scholars of usul al-fiqh and usul al-din (theologians), and its inspiration is the Book of Allah. This roots it in revelation, not human reason alone.
4
Ask why this distinction matters
Consider why the author would need to clarify this. Many might think Islamic reasoning borrowed from Greek philosophy. This passage guards against that misconception.
