Qiyas in Islamic Theology: Definition, Principles, and Method
Buti defines qiyas, distinguishes it from Greek logic, states its two principles, and explains induction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic concept of qiyas as distinct from Greek logic, its two foundational principles (causality and cosmic harmony), and the essential role of induction in establishing certain knowledge.

Core Idea
Islamic qiyas is not Greek logic; it is a method rooted in causality and induction from the Qur'an.

Buti begins by clarifying that the qiyas used in Islamic theology is not the Aristotelian syllogism familiar from Greek philosophy. Instead, it is a method derived from the Qur'an and employed by Islamic jurists and theologians. This passage defines qiyas, explains its two axiomatic principles, and describes how induction is necessary to apply it correctly.

Guided Path
1
Understand the distinction
Read the opening lines where Buti explicitly distinguishes Islamic qiyas from Greek logical syllogism. Note that Islamic qiyas is based on extracting causes, not on formal propositional forms.
This distinction is crucial to avoid confusion between the two methods.
2
Identify the two principles
Find the law of causality (every effect has a cause) and the law of harmony and order (the universe's diverse phenomena are connected by universal causes). Both are presented as self-evident axioms.
Buti emphasizes that these principles need no proof; they are intellectual axioms.
3
Grasp the role of induction
See how induction (istiqra') is the tool that reveals the cause and establishes constant relations. The passage describes the process of tracing particulars and observing constant accompaniment and reversal.
Complete induction is required, meaning you must examine all relevant particulars.
4
Recognize the condition for certainty
Buti warns that mere constant concomitance (cause and effect always together) is not enough; you must also verify that the cause is truly effective through certain proof, as coincidence or another factor could be involved.
This condition is especially important when building creed and certain knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلك الثاني القياس : وليس المقصود به القياس المنطقي المقتبس من الفلسفة اليونانية ، والقائم على القضايا والأشكال ، إنما المقصود به ذلك القياس الذي اصطلح عليه علماء أصول الفقه الإسلامي وعلماء أصول الدين ( المتكلمون ) بعد أن استلهموه من كتاب الله عز وجل . وهو منهج يتلخص في استخراج علة الشيء أو سببه ، ثم تلمسه فيما قد يشبهه من الأشياء المجهولة ، حتى إذا استيقن الباحث اشتراك كل من المعلوم والمجهول في علة واحدة ، قاس الثاني على الأول في حكمه المنبثق من تأثير تلك العلة . وتقوم فكرة القياس على مبدأين اثنين ، كل منهما من المسلمات العقلية التي لا تحتاج إلى برهان عليها : المبدأ الأول : قانون العلية ، أي إن لكل معلول علة ولكل أثر مؤثراً . المبدأ الثاني : قانون التناسق والنظام في العالم ، أي إن المظاهر الجزئية للكون وإن اختلفت أشكالها ، ترتبط بعلل كلية من شأنها أن تثبت التناسق والانسجام فيما بينها ، ومهما أوغلت في التدقيق بطبائع هذه العلل رأيتها تتجمع أخيراً في أقل عدد من العلل والأسباب . وإنما ينقدح القياس من هذين المبدأين أيضاً ، بواسطة الاستقراء ، إذ هو الذي يبصّر الباحث بحقيقة العلة ، ثم هو الذي يمكن بواسطته إدراك العلاقات الثابتة الكلية بين الأشياء المتناثرة أو المختلفة في الظاهر . وهكذا نلاحظ أن الاستقراء التام شرط أساسي لابد منه لكل من برهاني التلازم والقياس . وكيفية الاستقراء هنا هي أن تتتبع جزئيات ما ادعيت أنه علة لأمر معين ، فتجده لا ينفك عن إنتاج معلوله ، وذلك بأن تتأمل العلاقة بين العلة والمعلول فترى فيها ظاهرة الاطراد والانعكاس . أي كلما وجدت العلة وجد المعلول ، وكلما فقدت العلة فقد المعلول . ثم تمعن النظر بعد ذلك في العلة فتراها مؤثرة في المعلول بالبرهان اليقيني ، إذ قد يكون هذا الاطراد أو الانعكاس بينهما لمحض المصادفة أو لعامل آخر . وبذلك تعلم أن شرط القياس هنا ( أي في بناء العقيدة والقضايا اليقينية )
The second method: qiyas (analogical reasoning). What is intended by it is not the logical syllogism borrowed from Greek philosophy, which is based on propositions and figures; rather, what is intended is that qiyas which the scholars of Usul al-Fiqh and the scholars of Usul al-Din (the mutakallimun) agreed upon after deriving it from the Book of God Almighty. It is a method that consists in extracting the cause or reason of a thing, then seeking it in what may resemble it among unknown things, until when the researcher is certain of the sharing of both the known and the unknown in one cause, he analogises the second to the first in its ruling emanating from the influence of that cause. The idea of qiyas is based on two principles, each of which is among the intellectual axioms that need no proof: the first principle: the law of causality (qanun al-‘illiyya), i.e., every effect has a cause and every trace has an influence. The second principle: the law of harmony and order in the world (qanun al-tanasuq wa al-nizam fi al-‘alam), i.e., the partial manifestations of the universe, although their forms differ, are connected by universal causes that establish harmony and coherence among them, and no matter how deep you go into scrutiny of the natures of these causes, you see them finally gathering into the smallest number of causes and reasons. Qiyas arises from these two principles also by means of induction (istiqra’), for it is what clarifies to the researcher the reality of the cause, and then it is that by means of which it is possible to perceive the constant universal relations among scattered or apparently different things. Thus we note that complete induction (al-istiqra’ al-tamm) is an essential condition necessary for both the proof of implication (talawum) and qiyas. And the manner of induction here is that you trace the particulars of what you claim is a cause for a certain matter, and you find that it does not cease to produce its effect, and that by reflecting on the relation between cause and effect you see in it the phenomenon of constant accompaniment and reversal: i.e., whenever the cause is found, the effect is found, and whenever the cause is absent, the effect is absent. Then you deeply reflect on the cause and see it as effective in the effect by certain proof, because this constant accompaniment or reversal could be due to pure coincidence or another factor. And thus you know that the condition for qiyas here (i.e., in building creed and certain propositions)
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage defines the second method: qiyas (analogical reasoning). The author first distinguishes it from the Aristotelian logical syllogism; Islamic qiyas is derived from the Qur’an and used by jurists and theologians. The method consists of extracting the cause (‘illa) of a known thing, then looking for that same cause in an unknown thing, and once certainty is reached that both share the cause, the ruling of the known is applied to the unknown. But this procedure rests on two self-evident principles: (1) the law of causality: every effect has a cause; (2) the law of order: the universe is harmoniously connected by universal causes that reduce to a few. Qiyas also requires induction (istiqra’), which reveals the cause and establishes constant relations. Complete induction is essential: you must examine particulars and observe constant concomitance (whenever the cause exists, the effect exists) and reversal (when the cause is absent, the effect is absent). However, this pattern alone is insufficient; you must also verify that the cause is truly effective through certain proof, because concomitance might be coincidental. The passage ends by noting that this condition is especially important for building creed and certain knowledge.

Key Terms
القياس
العلية
الاستقراء
العلة
المعلول
التناسق
النظام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think qiyas in kalam is the same as Aristotelian syllogism.
Correction: Buti explicitly states that Islamic qiyas is not the logical syllogism borrowed from Greek philosophy. It is a method based on causality and induction, derived from the Qur'an.

Checkpoint
What are the two principles on which qiyas is based according to Buti?
A) The law of contradiction and the law of excluded middle
B) The law of causality and the law of harmony and order in the world
C) The principle of identity and the principle of sufficient reason
D) The law of non-contradiction and the law of causality

Key Takeaway
Qiyas in Islamic theology is a method of analogical reasoning based on the law of causality and cosmic order, requiring complete induction and certain proof of causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim to have certain knowledge about your Creator or your faith, have you personally verified the causes behind that belief through a rigorous method like qiyas, or are you relying on patterns that might be coincidental? Will you commit to tracing every core belief back to its cause?
?
The law of harmony and order says the universe is coherent and its causes reduce to a few. Is your own spiritual life coherent, or is it fragmented by contradictions? What one contradiction in your practice will you resolve today to align with this principle?
?
Buti states that complete induction—examining all particulars—is necessary for certain knowledge in creed. Are there areas of your deen where you have accepted a teaching without fully investigating its evidence? Name one and decide now: will you fill that gap, or will you remain willfully ignorant?

Three Conditions for Certain Qiyas
Defines the three conditions for qiyas to yield certainty in creedal matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three essential conditions for analogical reasoning (qiyas) to produce certainty in creedal matters, and why probabilistic analogy is insufficient for theology.

Core Idea
Certain analogy requires an effective, constant, and clear cause.

In this passage, Ramadan al-Buti outlines the rigorous standards required for analogical reasoning when dealing with matters of creed. He explains that for an analogy to yield certainty, the cause ('illa) must meet three strict criteria. If any condition is lacking, the analogy is only probabilistic and cannot be used in theology, though it may be acceptable in practical legal rulings. The passage also provides a vivid example to illustrate how this reasoning works in practice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and translation once to get the overall argument. Notice the three conditions and the example.
2
Identify the three conditions
Look for the three conditions listed: effectiveness (ta'thir), constancy and reversibility (itrad wa in'ikas), and clarity and precision (indibat). Understand each one.
The footnote clarifies what is meant by 'effectiveness'.
3
Distinguish between certain and probabilistic analogy
Notice how the author distinguishes between qiyas yaqini (certain) and qiyas dhanni (probabilistic). Understand why only the former is acceptable in creedal matters.
4
Explore the example
Work through the example of seeing dwellings and inferring water. See how the conditions are met: water is effective, constant, and clear.
This helps solidify the abstract conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هو أن تكون العلة مؤثرة في المعلول ) ، وأن تكون مطردة ومنعكسة وأن تكون منضبطة واضحة غير مضطربة .
فإذا تدانت العلة عن مستوى هذا الشرط ، كأن لم يتضح فيها التأثير ، وإنما تجلت فيها ملاءمة ما مع المعلول ، فهو قياس ظني ، لا يقبل في الأحكام الاعتقادية والعقلية ، وإنما يمكن أن يقبل في المسائل الفقهية العملية ، لقيام الدليل القاطع على أن الأدلة الظنية فيها كافية للتعبد والأحكام الشرعية كما أسلفنا ، فيكفي في القياس للأحكام الشرعية العملية أن تكون العلة منضبطة ومطردة منعكسة ، ولكن لا يشترط فيها أن تكون مؤثرة بل يكفي أن تكون ملائمة في اجتهاد الباحث لبناء حكم عليها . فالقياس في الشرعيات العملية يختلف في الحقيقة اختلافاً كبيراً عن طبيعة القياس وشروطه في المسائل الاعتقادية . مثال ذلك أن تبصر على البعد دوراً أو خياماً يسكنها الناس ، فتستيقن من ذلك وجود الماء في ذلك المكان فإن سبيل اليقين بذلك ، أنك تستحضر في ذهنك بسرعة خاطفة سائر ما تعلمه من الأماكن التي يعيش فيها الناس ، فترى أن من أهم أسباب صلاحيتها لمقامهم ، توافر الماء فيها ؛ لا يختلف هذا المعنى بحال من الأحوال ، كما تلاحظ تأثير السبب الذي هو الماء في المسبب الذي هو العيش وإمكان الحياة
وعندئذ تقيس هذا المكان الذي يلوح لك من بعيد على تلك الأماكن الأخرى وتجزم بوجود الماء فيه وإن لم تره عيناك
.
(۱) نقصد بالتأثير أن يثبت بالبرهان سببية العلة للمعلول ، كسببية المطر للإنبات والنار للإحراق ، بقطع النظر عن البحث في حقيقة هذه السببية وتحليلها على ضوء الإيمان بالمسب الحقيقي جل جلاله ومكان البحث في تحليل ذلك ، الحديث عن قانون السببية في الكون فارجع إليه في مكانه من هذا الكتاب
It (the cause) must be effective in the effect, and must be constant and reversible, and must be well-defined, clear, and not ambiguous. If the cause falls short of this condition, such that its effectiveness is not clear, and only some suitability with the effect appears, then it is a probabilistic analogy (qiyas dhanni), which is not accepted in creedal and rational judgments. Rather, it can be accepted in practical jurisprudential matters, because decisive evidence has established that probabilistic evidences are sufficient for worship and legal rulings, as we have mentioned earlier. So for analogy in practical legal rulings, it is sufficient that the cause be well-defined, constant, and reversible, but it is not required that it be effective; rather, it is sufficient that it be suitable in the researcher's ijtihad for building a ruling upon it. Thus analogy in practical legal matters actually differs greatly from the nature of analogy and its conditions in creedal matters. An example of that: you see at a distance a house or tents inhabited by people, and you become certain of the presence of water in that place. The way to certainty in that is that you bring to mind in a flash all the places you know where people live, and you see that one of the most important reasons for their suitability for habitation is the availability of water; this meaning does not differ in any way. Moreover, you observe the effect of the cause, which is water, in the effect, which is living and the possibility of life. Then you analogize this place that appears to you from afar to those other places, and you affirm the presence of water in it even though you have not seen it with your own eyes. (1) By 'effect' we mean that it is proven by demonstration that the cause is causal for the effect, like the causality of rain for growth and fire for burning, regardless of the inquiry into the reality of this causality and its analysis in light of belief in the True Cause (Exalted be His Majesty). The place for research on that analysis is the discussion on the law of causality in the universe; refer to it in its place in this book.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions that must be met for analogical reasoning (qiyas) to yield certainty in creedal and rational matters. The author, Ramadan al-Buti, states three essential conditions for the cause ('illa): it must be effective (mu'aththira) in the effect, meaning proven causality; it must be constant and reversible (mutarida wa mun'akisa), applying in all cases and not contradicted; and it must be well-defined and clear (mundabita waadiha), not ambiguous. If the cause does not fully meet these conditions, the analogy is probabilistic (dhanni) and cannot be used in creedal or intellectual matters, only in practical legal rulings where probabilistic evidence is sufficient. The author then gives an example: seeing dwellings in the distance leads to certain knowledge of water because the cause (water) is effective for human habitation. The reasoning involves recalling known places where water is essential. The footnote clarifies that 'effectiveness' here refers to proven causality by demonstration, setting aside the deeper theological debate about the ultimate Cause (Allah).

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All analogies are equally valid and yield certainty.
Correction: Only analogies with a cause that is effective, constant, and clear produce certainty; those with merely suitable causes are probabilistic and not valid in creedal matters.

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the main difference between qiyas in creedal matters and qiyas in practical legal matters?
A) In legal matters, the cause must be effective, while in creed it needs only suitability.
B) In creedal matters, the cause must be effective, constant, and well-defined to yield certainty, while in legal matters, suitability and constancy are sufficient.
C) Both require the same conditions.
D) In legal matters, qiyas is not allowed.

Key Takeaway
For analogy to yield certainty in creedal matters, the cause must be effective, constant, and clear; otherwise, it is only probabilistic and unsuitable for theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold about Islam that you have not thoroughly examined. On what kind of evidence does it rest—certain or probabilistic? Are you willing to test it against the three conditions of effective, constant, and clear cause? What would change if you did?
?
You routinely make judgments about people and situations using analogies. When was the last time you assumed something about someone based on a similarity to a past experience? Did you treat that assumption as certain or probable? How might the distinction between certain and probabilistic analogy change the way you judge others?
?
The author says probabilistic analogy is acceptable in matters of worship but not in matters of creed. Consider: is there any aspect of your personal faith—something you believe about Allah, the Prophet, or the afterlife—that you have accepted based on a mere suitability or feeling rather than a clear, constant, effective proof? What would it cost you to scrutinize it now?

Certain vs Probabilistic Qiyas
Contrasts probabilistic and certain qiyas with two examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between probabilistic and certain qiyas through the water and design examples.

Core Idea
Certain qiyas requires necessary causality, not mere suitability.

This lesson clarifies a critical distinction in Islamic reasoning: when does analogy yield certainty? The author contrasts two scenarios—inferring people from water (mere conjecture) and inferring a Maker from design (certain). This distinction is foundational for evaluating theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the reversed water example
Read the passage where seeing water leads to inferring people. Note why this is only probabilistic: the causality is not effective but merely suitable.
This contrasts with the earlier certain example of inferring water from people.
2
Study the design argument
Examine the statement that the mark of craftsmanship indicates a Maker, because the effect cannot separate from its cause. This is certain qiyas.
This is a classic kalam argument for God's existence.
3
Compare the two types of qiyas
Identify the key difference: necessary causation (design) vs mere appropriateness (water-people). Certain qiyas requires a real causal link.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما لو عكست النظر في هذا المثال ذاته. كأن أبصرت على البعد بريق ماء ، ففهمت من درجة اليقين ذلك وجود أناس يعيشون هناك ؛ فإنه مجرد ظن لا يمكن أن يرقى إلى ذلك
وذلك لأن علية الماء لحياة الإنسان حقيقة ثابتة بدلالة التأثير فلابد من وجود الماء حيث يوجد الناس . أما علية الماء لوجود الناس من حوله فمحض
مناسبة وملاءمة
ومثاله أيضاً ، دلالة كل مافيه مظهر الصنعة والتدبير ، على وجود صانع ومدبّر له . ضرورة أن المعلول لا ينفك عن علته
ومن هنا تعلم أن علماء المسلمين إنما يتبعون المنهج الاستقرائي في كل مالم يمكن إخضاعه للتجربة والمشاهدة ، وفي ظل هذا المنهج يلتقي كل من الالتزام والقياس . وهو كما ترى أبعد ما يكون عن الاستنتاجات الغيبية والتأملات المجردة التي أوغلت فيها الفلسفة اليونانية أيما إيغال .
بل قد علم كل من تأمل في المنهج الإسلامي للبحث ، أن علماء الإسلام لا يمكن أن يقيموا أي حكم عقلي أو عقيدي إلا على أساس الحقيقة التي تجمعت فيها كل مقومات اليقين
•
فأما تلك الحقائق الأخرى التي ظلت مستورة وراء حجاب الشكوك ولم تطلها إلا يد الاستنتاج النظري ، كتلك التي تلوح خلال دراسات تاريخية أو آثار مكتشفة أو مستحاثات قديمة - فما عرف التاريخ الإسلامي أن حقيقة يقينية ما ، قد أقيمت فوقها أو أنها اتخذت برهان نقد أو استدلال أو بناء فكر . ولكنها ظلت عندهم بحثاً غير موصول وشكاً يطوف حولـه كل احتمال ، وسبيلاً يدعو لمواصلة السير إلى نهايته بخطاً من البحث الاستقرائي السديد

✩
خلاصة سريعة جداً ، عن المنهج العلمي للبحث عند المسلمين
As for if you reverse the consideration in this same example, such that you see at a distance the glimmer of water, and you understand from that with certainty the presence of people living there; then it is mere conjecture (dhann) that cannot rise to certainty. That is because the causality of water for human life is a proven truth by indication of effectiveness, so water must exist wherever people are. As for the causality of water for the presence of people around it, it is mere appropriateness and suitability. Another example of it is the indication of everything that contains the mark of craftsmanship and design, to the existence of a Maker and Designer for it, necessarily because the effect cannot be separated from its cause. From here you learn that Muslim scholars follow the inductive method (al-manhaj al-istiqra'i) in everything that cannot be subjected to experiment and observation. Under this method, both certain knowledge (iltizam) and analogy (qiyas) meet. And it is, as you see, far removed from the occult speculations and abstract contemplations that Greek philosophy indulged in excessively. Rather, everyone who has reflected on the Islamic method of research knows that Muslim scholars cannot establish any rational or creedal judgment except on the basis of truth that has gathered all elements of certainty. As for those other truths that have remained veiled behind the screen of doubts and have not been reached except by the hand of theoretical deduction, such as those that appear through historical studies, discovered artifacts, or ancient fossils, Islamic history has never known that a certain truth was established upon them or that they were taken as proof for criticism, inference, or intellectual construction. Rather, they remained for them an unresolved research and a doubt around which every possibility hovers, and a path that calls for continuing the journey to its end with steps of sound inductive research. That is the very brief summary of the scientific method of research according to Muslims.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of qiyas by contrasting certain and probabilistic analogy. The author presents a reversed scenario: seeing water and inferring people is only probabilistic, because the causality is not effective—people may not be present near every body of water. He then gives the classic argument for God's existence: the design in the universe indicates a Maker, because the effect (design) necessarily implies a cause (designer). This is certain qiyas. The author then explains the Islamic inductive method (manhaj istiqra'i), which combines certain knowledge (iltizam) and analogy (qiyas). He emphasizes that this method is far from Greek philosophical speculation. Muslim scholars only base judgments on truths that have all elements of certainty (yakin). They reject speculative historical evidence, such as artifacts or fossils, as insufficient for certainty, considering them as unresolved research that requires further inductive investigation. The passage concludes by calling this a brief summary of the Islamic scientific method.

Key Terms
قياس ظني
قياس يقيني
مظهر الصنعة
ضَرُورَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is inferring people from the glimmer of water only conjecture?
A) Because water is not necessary for life.
B) Because the causality of water for the presence of people is mere suitability, not effective causation.
C) Because people never live near water.
D) Because the example is reversed.

Key Takeaway
Certain qiyas requires a necessary causal link, whereas probabilistic qiyas relies on mere suitability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the world around you, do you rely on mere suitability (like the water example) or on necessary causation (like the design example) to recognize the Creator? What would change if you treated the evidence as merely probable?
?
Your daily life is filled with inferences: from patterns to causes. Which of your beliefs about God rest on necessary reasoning, and which on mere appropriateness? Are you willing to examine each?
?
If the existence of design necessarily implies a Designer, why do you sometimes live as if the world runs on its own? What would it look like to truly live as if every crafted thing cries out for its Maker?

The Islamic Inductive Method
Explains the Islamic inductive method and its epistemic standards.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic inductive method and why Muslim scholars reject speculative history as a basis for certainty.

Core Idea
Islamic method builds on certain evidence, not abstract speculation.

After distinguishing certain and probabilistic qiyas, the author now explains the broader Islamic inductive method (al-manhaj al-istiqra'i). This method combines certainty and analogy while rejecting Greek speculative philosophy. He also outlines what kinds of evidence are acceptable for establishing creedal truths.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Islamic inductive method
Read how the method applies to things beyond experiment: it combines certain knowledge (iltizam) and analogy (qiyas) and avoids occult speculation.
The author contrasts this with Greek philosophy.
2
Examine the requirement of certainty
Note that Muslim scholars only base rulings on truths that 'gather all elements of certainty.' This excludes theoretical deduction.
3
Analyze the rejection of speculative history
Read the list of uncertain evidence: historical studies, artifacts, fossils. These are considered unresolved research, not proof.
This shows the strict epistemological standard.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Islamic methodology accepts speculative historical evidence as certain proof for creed.
Correction: The author explicitly states that such evidence is not used for certain judgments; it is only a path for further inductive research.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what status do historical studies, artifacts, and fossils have in Islamic methodology?
A) They are considered certain proofs.
B) They are treated as unresolved research open to further inductive study.
C) They are rejected entirely.
D) They are used as primary evidence for creedal judgments.

Key Takeaway
The Islamic inductive method only accepts truths with all elements of certainty, treating speculative evidence as unresolved research.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author says Muslim scholars never based a certain truth on speculative historical evidence. Think of a belief you hold about Islamic history that you consider absolutely certain. What is the foundation of that certainty? Is it based on transmitted texts with all elements of certainty, or on academic theories and interpretations? If the latter, will you reclassify that belief as 'unresolved research' rather than a settled creed?
?
This passage sets a high bar: only truths with all elements of certainty can justify a rational or creedal judgment. When you argue about religion with others, do you often rely on evidence that is actually speculative (e.g., a historical claim or a philosophical argument)? Are you willing to only present arguments that meet this standard, even if it means being silent on many issues? What would that cost you?
?
The source calls speculative evidence 'a path that calls for continuing the journey to its end with steps of sound inductive research.' Is there an area of your knowledge—perhaps about the early Muslim community, or the nature of existence—where you have settled for probability instead of pursuing certainty? What concrete step will you take this week to move that topic from doubt towards sound inductive investigation?
